201 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Rini v. New York C. R. Co.


Nos. 198, 199, 200, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


429 Pa. 235; 240 A.2d 372; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 795


November 14, 1967, Argued  
March 15, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from orders of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1966, Nos. 557 and 668, and Jan. T., 1967, No. 861, in cases of Guy J. Rini v. New York Central Railroad Company; Edward L. Brant v. Same; and James Masterson v. Same.



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review of the orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee railroad's motions to dismiss appellants' actions for personal injuries against appellee under the doctrine of forum non conveniens.


OVERVIEW: Three appellant employees brought separate suits against appellee railroad for personal injuries sustained during the scope of their employment, and the lower court granted appellee's motions to dismiss appellants' actions under the doctrine of forum non conveniens. The court affirmed, because appellants' causes of action arose outside of the Commonwealth, neither appellants nor any of the witnesses reside in or have any connection with the county, nor are the witnesses within subpoena range of the lower court. The court found that each appellant had recourse to another appropriate forum and that the applicable statute of limitations had not expired. The court concluded that no abuse of discretion existed on the part of the lower court in dismissing these actions under the doctrine of forum non conveniens.


OUTCOME: The orders of the lower court that granted appellee railroad's motions to dismiss appellant employees' actions for personal injuries against appellee under the doctrine of forum non conveniens was affirmed, because appellants and their claims had no connection with the Commonwealth, other appropriate forums existed, and the statutes of limitations had not expired.

 

202 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Harrington v. Carroll


No. 203, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


428 Pa. 510; 239 A.2d 437; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 917


January 3, 1968, Argued  
March 14, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Nov. T., 1967, No. 1331, in case of Clark J. Harrington et al. v. Vincent A. Carroll et al.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, taxpayer and elector, on behalf of other taxpayers and electors, and by the Republican city committee on behalf of its unsuccessful candidate, sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee judges', acting as an election return board, objections to appellants' complaint relative to the election of a councilman.


OVERVIEW: Candidate was elected for the office of councilman-at-large of city council. Appellee judges, who acted as the return board, were prepared to certify his election. Appellants, taxpayer and elector, on behalf of other taxpayers and electors, and by the Republican city committee on behalf of its defeated candidate, filed a complaint in equity. Appellants prayed that the lower court, by mandatory injunction, order appellees to certify the election of the defeated party on the averment he received the highest number of votes of the unsuccessful candidates for councilman-at-large, or, that in the alternative, the court direct the incoming president of city council to call a special election to fill the vacancy if winner was declared ineligible. Appellees filed preliminary objections to the complaint, which the lower court sustained. The court affirmed the lower court's decision because the city council had exclusive jurisdiction to determine the qualifications of its members, including the issue of the winning candidate's residence, which was according to legislative mandate under the Philadelphia Home Rule Charter, § 2-103.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision which sustained appellee judges', acting as an election return board, objections to appellants', taxpayer and elector, on behalf of other taxpayers and electors, and by the Republican city committee on behalf of its unsuccessful candidate, complaint because the city council had exclusive jurisdiction to determine the issue of the winning candidate's residence, under the Philadelphia Home Rule Charter.

 

203 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Pennsylvania Soc. for Prevention of Cruelty to Animals v. Bravo Enterprises, Inc.


No. 215, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


428 Pa. 350; 237 A.2d 342; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 896


May 1, 1967, Argued  
January 18, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Granted October 3, 1967. Reargued November 30, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas No. 3 of Philadelphia County, March T., 1966, No. 2810, in case of Pennsylvania Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals and Women's Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals v. Bravo Enterprises, Inc. and Jimmy Toppi.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, entertainment corporation, sought review of a decree in equity from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which enjoined appellant from conducting a performance known as the International Festival of Matadors and Bulls, an exhibition of American style bullfighting. Appellee, animal rights organization, brought an action to enjoin the performance.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, animal rights organization, sought to enjoin a performance by appellant, entertainment corporation, known as the International Festival of Matadors and Bulls. The lower court found in favor of appellee. On appeal, the court held that the baiting and otherwise cruelly ill-treating aspects of the cruelty to animals statute (statute), 18 P.S. § 4942, were susceptible to a wide sphere of interpretation. The court concluded that the performance did not fall within the exceptions to the general rule that equity would not enjoin the mere commission of a crime, and therefore equity could properly exercise its jurisdiction. The court determined that there was nothing in the statute that expressly gave appellee the power or authority to institute the action in equity nor would the court read into the statute something that was not written therein. The court found that appellee did not enjoy any greater property right in the prevention of such offenses and did not suffer injury to any greater degree than the general public when violations of the statute occurred. The court held that appellee had no standing and reversed the decree of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree enjoining appellant, entertainment corporation, from conducting its performance. The court held that appellee, animal rights organization, had no standing under the cruelty to animals statute because appellee did not expressly have the power to institute the action, did not enjoy a greater property right in the prevention of such offenses, and did not suffer injury to any greater degree than the general public.

206 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


McConnell Appeal


Nos. 110, 111, 112, 113, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


428 Pa. 270; 236 A.2d 796; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 885


January 18, 1967, Argued  
January 3, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Nos. 12677, 12678, 12679 and 12680 of 1966, in re approval of bond of the Manufacturers Light and Heat Company and surety under right of eminent domain.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, four property owners, challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which approved four condemnation bonds in unlimited amounts with approved surety, in connection with appellee natural gas company's condemnation of easements across the land of appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellee natural gas company's order was granted approving condemnation bonds in unlimited amounts with approved surety, regarding the condemnation of easements across the land of appellants, four property owners. Exceptions were filed alleging the takings were unreasonable, arbitrary, and capricious. The state supreme court vacated the order, with leave to appellants to file preliminary objections within 30 days of the date of the filing of the opinion, pursuant to the Eminent Domain Code of 1964. Appellee averred that it had eminent domain authority by reason of the Natural Gas Companies Act, 15 P.S. § 3549, which did not include any appeal procedure. The code displaced the courts of equity as the forum for condemnation matters. Section 604 of the code provided that within 30 days after service of the notice of condemnation, the condemnee may file preliminary objections to the declaration of taking. Section 901 of the code did preserve parts of the act. But, in a case of condemnation under the act, the timeliness of the appeal was determined by the code (30 days), and must follow the code for procedural safeguards, finding no recourse in a court of equity.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated the lower court's order, against appellee natural gas company approving condemnation bonds with leave to appellants, four property owners, to file preliminary objections within 30 days of the date of the filing of the opinion, under the Eminent Domain Code of 1964 because the code displaced the courts of equity as the forum for condemnation matters and provided procedural safeguards.

 

207 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Bertera's Hopewell Foodland, Inc. v. Masters


No. 258, March Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


428 Pa. 20; 236 A.2d 197; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 443


September 27, 1967, Argued  
November 28, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Sept. T., 1965, No. 5, in case of Bertera's Hopewell Foodland, Inc. v. Robert J. Masters, District Attorney, John A. Krzton, Chief County Detective, Louis Polce et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff supermarket sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania) in favor of defendant district attorney, denying the supermarket's request for an injunction to restrain the district attorney from enforcing the Pennsylvania Sunday closing laws.


OVERVIEW: The supermarket operated a store with more than 10 employees. The Pennsylvania Sunday Closing Law, Act of September 27, 1961, 1961 Pa. Laws 1695, prohibited operations on Sunday only by stores with no more than nine employees or owned and operated by members of an immediate family. The trial court found the statute to be constitutional, and the court agreed. The court noted that the purpose of the statute to set one day apart from all others as a day of rest, repose, recreation, and tranquility. The court held that the whole structure of government was founded and operated upon the principle of a belief in a Supreme Being. The court also held that it would be a violation of the U.S. Const. amend. I to compel people, who accept Sunday as a religious day, to work on that day. The could also noted that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague just because the legislature properly drew a line at nine employees, and further that it would be unjust to shut down family owned stores, while allowing the operation of marts which employed nine persons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of the district attorney.

 

208 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Ehret Estate


No. 196, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


427 Pa. 584; 235 A.2d 414; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 524


May 3, 1967, Argued  
November 14, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Orphans' Court of Philadelphia County, April T., 1914, No. 116, in re estate of Michael Ehret, deceased.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a trust administration matter, the trustee claimed an interim commission on principal for ordinary services in the Orphans' Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania). The guardian ad litem objected to the payment of any interim commission, and the lower court disallowed this credit in the account. The trustee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The sole issue before the court was whether a trustee under a testamentary trust created prior to 1945, which was not an executor, could have received for its ordinary services as trustee an interim commission on principal. The court noted that, under prior law, where the same fiduciary was both executor and trustee and had received, for its ordinary services in both capacities, a commission on principal at the termination of its executorship, it could not have thereafter received an interim or an additional commission on principal for its ordinary services in its dual capacity. The court held that a situation where the corporate trustee was never an executor and had never received any compensation or commission on principal for its services, was clearly distinguishable and the broad language in earlier cases had to be limited to the facts therein. The court noted that there was no statute which directly and specifically prohibited an interim commission on principal for ordinary services under the facts presented and concluded that it should have allowed, if earned, the payment of a fair and reasonable interim commission on principal to a non-executor trustee of a long-term trust.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decree.

210 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Snyder


No. 83, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


427 Pa. 83; 233 A.2d 530; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 462


January 4, 1967, Submitted  
September 26, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Oyer and Terminer and General Jail Delivery of Montgomery County, Nov. T., 1960, No. 181, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. George M. Snyder.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed with instructions. *



* Reporter's Note: On March 11, 1968, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Oyer and Terminer and General Jail Delivery of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which denied his motion for post conviction relief, after he was convicted of voluntary manslaughter and his conviction was affirmed on appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter. His conviction was affirmed on appeal. He filed a petition for post conviction relief. The lower court denied his petition. He appealed from the denial, arguing that four coerced "confessions" were introduced at his trial. The court affirmed, holding that the trial counsel's failure to object to the confessions waived any claim for coerced confession. The trial counsel's deliberate bypass of the claim was imputed to appellant because there was no showing of incompetency or the presence of exceptional circumstances. Thus, the court noted that the trial counsel's decision not to attack the confession bound his client. However, the court held that there was an error in the computation of the effective date of appellant's sentence, which resulted in the imposition of a sentence for voluntary manslaughter which exceeded the statutory maximum. The court held that appellant was clearly entitled to credit for the time served after imposition of sentence and before the supersedeas was granted. Credit was also allowed for the ten day period prior to the grant of bail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant motion for post conviction relief, subsequent to his conviction for voluntary manslaughter. However, the court held that there was an error in the computation of sentence and that he was entitled to credit for time served after the imposition of sentence and before the supersedeas was granted.

Appeal of Massachusetts Mut. Life Ins. Co.


No. 7, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 566; 235 A.2d 790; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 607


November 28, 1966, Argued  
September 26, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused October 25, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1965, No. 3454, in re appeal of Massachusetts Mutual Life Insurance Company from assessment of Board of Property Assessment, Appeals and Review of Allegheny County.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a board, a city, and a school district, challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which refused the board's request for argument before the trial court en banc and dismissed the board's exceptions to the trial court's reduction in the assessment of appellee corporate taxpayer's property.


OVERVIEW: The trial court recognized as valid the study of an economist who testified on the taxpayer's behalf, and significantly reduced the board's triennial assessment based on this study. The city and the school district intervened in the appeal. The court found that the common level ratio testimony presented by the economist was competent, credible, and properly accepted by the trial court. The court found that it was proper to make an itemization between land and building by an assessor initially. The court specifically approved the procedure of valuing first, the entire property, second, the land and, then, allocating the balance to the building. The court noted that the primary duty of a court was to review the assessment on the whole property. Thereafter, if the parties could not agree on the division between land and buildings, this matter, too, could be considered but only after the assessment of the whole property had been determined. With respect to the financial impact, the court stated that no taxpayer should be asked to pay more than its fair share of taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

Appeal of Sixth Liberty, Inc.


Nos. 63, 64, 65, 66, 67, 68, 69, March Term, 1965, Nos. 11, 12, 13, 14, 15, 16, 17, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 589; 235 A.2d 801; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 615


November 28, 1966, Argued  
September 26, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused October 25, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from order of County Court of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1963, Nos. 862 to 868, inclusive, in re appeals of Sixth Liberty, Inc. and Four Picher Development Corp., from assessments of Board of Property Assessment, Appeals and Review of Allegheny County.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners and the Board of Property Assessment, Appeals, and Review of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), sought review of the judgment of the County Court of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which issued a mandamus order directing the Board to hear the owners' appeals from assessments of the owners' parcels for years between triennial years, and reassessed the parcels.


OVERVIEW: The Board refused to accept the owner's appeals from assessments on the ground that the appeal period had expired. The owners then filed a complaint in mandamus requesting an order directing the Board to hear the appeals. The trial court granted the writ of mandamus. The Board heard the appeals, but made no changes in the assessments. After the trial court reassessed the parcels, both the Board and the owners appealed. Following remand, the trial court reaffirmed its earlier orders. The court held that: (1) the trial court erred in granting the writ of mandamus because the assessor did not reassess the property as required by § 13, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5452.13, of the Act of June 21, 1939, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, §§ 5452.1-.20, and the owners did not request a reassessment prior to the first Monday of September of an inter-triennial year, as required by § 13; (2) the Board was not entitled to question the trial court's erroneous order on appeal because the Board did not file an appeal within the statutory time limits; and (3) the trial court was required to reassess the properties because unrebutted, credible expert testimony of value was improperly rejected.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order directing the Board to hear the property owners' appeals and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to enter an order directing the Board to correct the assessments of the subject property in accordance with the opinion.

Commonwealth ex rel. Robinson v. Myers


No. 17, May Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


427 Pa. 104; 233 A.2d 220; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 463


May 22, 1967, Submitted  
September 26, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of York County, Aug. T., 1964, No. 372, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Harvey Robinson v. David N. Myers, Superintendent.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which ruled in favor of appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania in habeas corpus proceedings that the inmate's failure to perfect an appeal within the time allowed by law did not result from an unconstitutional deprivation of the assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted and sentenced for voluntary manslaughter but no post-trial motions were filed and no appeal was taken. The inmate's trial counsel was pleased with the verdict because the inmate had not been convicted of murder in the first degree. Counsel mistakenly informed the inmate that a retrial could result in his conviction for murder. The court reversed the lower court's decision because the conviction for manslaughter served as an acquittal on the charge of murder in the first degree and it was clear that the inmate's acquiescence in counsel's recommendation not to appeal was based upon an inadequate appreciation of the consequences of such action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision and remanded the record with directions that the lower court appoint counsel for the purpose of filing post-trial motions and, if necessary, prosecuting an appeal. In the event post-trial motions are denied, the court would permit, upon motion of counsel, an appeal to be docketed as if timely filed and thereafter orally argued.

Madway v. Board for the Assessment & Revision of Taxes


Nos. 90, 91, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


427 Pa. 138; 233 A.2d 273; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 470


January 16, 1967, Argued  
September 26, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 64-12655, in re appeals of Lower Merion Township and School District from Montgomery County Board for the Assessment and Revision of Taxes.

Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 64-12696, in case of Harry K. Madway, Sam Madway, Ralph K. Madway et al., trading as Madway Engineers and Constructors v. Board for the Assessment and Revision of Taxes.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought a review of judgments from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) that affirmed an unfinished annual assessment by appellee, a county board of assessment and revision, which had assessed taxes on property upon which the taxpayers were constructing apartments and that declared amendments to statutes authorizing reassessments to reflect the value of new construction were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers contended that Pennsylvania First Class Township Code of June 24, 1931 (1931 P. L. 1206, § 1709.1, as amended by Pennsylvania Act of August 24, 1963 (1963 P. L. 1212, § 1 (Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 56709.1 (Supp. 1966)))), and Pennsylvania Public School Code of March 10, 1949 (1949 P. L. 30, § 677.1, as amended by Pennsylvania Act of August 8, 1963 (1968 P. L. 583, § 1 (Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 6-677.1 (Supp. 1966)))), (called Amendments) prohibited the assessment against their property until the property was conveyed or occupied. The board contended that the Amendments violated Pa. Const. art. IX, § 1 by subjecting different tax rates on the same class of property, which was real estate. The court determined that the Amendments were unconstitutional as determined by the trial court. The court held that the board was authorized to make an interim assessment on the property prior to the completion of the project, which could be subsequently reassessed upon completion of the apartments, which did not violate § 1 because the same rate was applied to the property as was applied to other real estate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court that affirmed one assessment by the board and reversed another assessment by the board.

 

215 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Lafferty


No. 23, May Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 541; 233 A.2d 256; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 603


May 25, 1967, Argued  
September 26, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 369 Commonwealth Docket, 1962, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Fred B. Lafferty, J. D. Lafferty and Charles W. Albright, Jr., partners, doing business as Lafferty Trucking Company.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant carrier sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed its appeal from a decision of the state finance board that sustained an assessment for a use tax deficiency, holding that the carrier was not engaged in a "public utility service" within the meaning of the Selective Sales and Use Tax Act (Act), 1955 Pa. Laws 1228, as amended, 72 Pa. Stat. § 3403.1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The carrier conducted a business of contract carrier by motor vehicle in both interstate and intrastate commerce. The state finance board sustained an assessment for a use tax deficiency against the carrier. The lower court dismissed the appeal. On further appeal, the court affirmed. The carrier asserted that because it performed services that could be equated with those of a genuine public utility, that is, a common carrier, it was encompassed by the Act's public service sales and use tax exclusion. However, an entity was required to be a "public utility" under the Public Utility Code to qualify for the sales and use tax public utility service exclusion. The cases the carrier cited in support dealt only with admitted public utilities. Although common carriers were specifically included within the definition of "public utility," contract carriers were excluded from the definition of "common carrier." Because the two classes of public utilities and nonpublic utilities were reasonably differentiated, the exclusion clause did not conflict with the uniformity clause, Pa. Const. art. IX, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that dismissed the carrier's appeal from a decision that sustained an assessment for a use tax deficiency.

 

216 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Pa. Human Relations Com v. Chester Sch. Dist.


No. 82, May Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


427 Pa. 157; 233 A.2d 290; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 473; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9799


April 24, 1967, Argued  
September 26, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, March T., 1967, No. 12, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 637 Commonwealth Docket, 1964, in case of Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission v. Chester School District.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed and record remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission sought review of the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the order of court of common pleas that was in favor of appellee school district.


OVERVIEW: After public hearings, the human rights commission ordered the school district to take steps to desegregate six public schools. The school district appealed and claimed that in the absence of a legislative directive, the school board need not consider race in drawing school boundary lines. The school district also claimed that the § 5(i)(1) of the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act, 43 P.S. § 955(i)(1), required an affirmative act by the school district. The court of common pleas held that unless the school district intentionally fostered or maintained segregation in the public schools, the human rights commission exceeded its jurisdiction in ordering the school district to take affirmative steps to relieve racial imbalance in its schools. The court of common pleas further held the findings relied upon by the human rights commission were arbitrary and capricious. The superior court affirmed. The court granted allocatur and reversed the decisions of the courts below. The court held that since the school district had the power to take corrective measures, its failure to act amounted to the continued withholding from minority children the advantages of an integrated education.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the lower courts, which was in favor of the school district, and reinstated the provisions of the human relations commission's order that were directed toward racial imbalance in the schools. The court also remanded the case to the human relations commission.

 

217 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Specter v. Martin


No. 339, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 102; 232 A.2d 729; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 552


May 2, 1967, Argued  
July 3, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reported at 426 Pa. 102 at 131.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, March T., 1967, No. 1400, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Arlen Specter, District Attorney v. Edward J. Martin, Finance Director.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city finance director sought review from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, which found that appellee district attorney was entitled to exercise the duties of district attorney while he was a candidate for mayor and directed the city finance director to pay the district attorney forthwith.


OVERVIEW: The city finance director argued that because the district attorney was a mayoral candidate, he could not continue to occupy the office of the district attorney and he was required to resign under Article X § 10-107(5) of the city's home rule charter. The court disagreed. The court found that the district attorney was not an officer of the city as to subject him to the provisions under the charter and that the removal provisions under the charter offended the state constitution. Although the district attorney was elected by voters of the city and paid from city funds, his duties were performed on behalf of the Commonwealth. Therefore the court held that he was not a city officer. He was also not a county officer because he did not perform his duties on behalf of a county. The court considered the district attorney to be a quasi-judicial officer. Under the state constitution, the district attorney could be removed from office upon a conviction of misbehavior in office. If he were subject to the charter, he would be liable to removal from office by a method offensive to the constitutionally ordained exclusive methods of removal of a constitutional officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment allowing the district attorney to continue in this capacity while he was a candidate for mayor.

 

218 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Specter v. Martin


No. 339, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 102; 232 A.2d 729; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 436



July 3, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 426 Pa. 102 at 131.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Opinion of July 3, 1967, Reported at 426 Pa. 102.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city finance director sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which required him to pay the salary of appellee district attorney because the district attorney was not a city officer and thus not required to resign his office when he became a candidate for election as mayor.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney brought a mandamus suit against the city finance director to compel payment of his salary, which had been stopped when the district attorney became a candidate for mayor. The trial court directed payment of the salary and ruled that the district attorney was a state officer and not prohibited by the city's home rule charter from running for election as mayor while remaining in office as prosecutor. On appeal, the finance director argued that Pa. Const. art. XIV, § 1 explicitly provided that district attorneys were county officers, that Pa. Const. art. XIV, § 8(1) abolished all county offices in Philadelphia and required the city to perform all county government functions, and that under Phila., Pa., Home Rule Charter art. X, § 10-107(5), no city officer or employee, except incumbents running for reelection, was permitted to be a candidate for any public office unless he resigned from his city office or employment. The district attorney argued that as a prosecutor he was a state officer and not subject to the Phila., Pa., Home Rule Charter. The court was evenly divided on the issue, and the trial court's decision therefore was left undisturbed.


OUTCOME: The court was evenly divided, leaving undisturbed the trial court's judgment holding that the district attorney was a state officer and requiring the finance director to pay the district attorney's salary during his mayoral candidacy.

 

219 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Bogert v. Allentown Housing Authority


No. 46, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 151; 231 A.2d 147; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 553


October 3, 1966, Argued  
June 29, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Sept. T., 1964, No. 22, in case of LeRoy S. Bogert, Francis R. Balliet, Stephen Banko et al. v. The Allentown Housing Authority et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which sustained that portion of the preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer and dismissed their complaint, reasoning that defendant Housing Authority (authority) was not subject to judicial restraint or under a duty to hold a public hearing, and permission to file an amended complaint would have been "fruitless."


OVERVIEW: The property owners filed an equity action against the authority due to the proposed construction of public housing. The property owners sought an injunction to restrain the authority from further proceeding with the proposed housing, to compel the authority to disclose the extent to which the property owners had become obligated in regard to the proposed housing, and to conduct an investigation to determine whether the property owners had been prejudiced, obligated, or harmed by any action of the authority. The authority filed preliminary objections including a motion for a more specific pleading. The trial court sustained that portion of the preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer and dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court reversed the decree and remanded to the trial court for the purpose of permitting the property owners to amend their complaint. The court found that, pursuant to the "Right to Know" statute, until the property owners' requested disclosure was made it could not be determined whether the actions of the authority were arbitrary and capricious, and thus amenable to judicial restraint, or beyond judicial restraint.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree of the trial court, and remanded for the purpose of permitting the property owners to amend their complaint to show that the actions on the part of the authority were subject to judicial restraint.

Fiumara v. Texaco, Inc.


No. 222, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 159; 231 A.2d 297; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 554


November 18, 1966, Argued  
June 29, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas No. 5 of Philadelphia County, June T., 1964, No. 4423, in case of Pasquale Fiumara v. Texaco, Inc., Sinclair Refining Company, Humble Oil & Refining Company et al.



DISPOSITION: Appeal quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff gasoline station owner appealed from a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which discharged the owner's rule upon defendant oil companies to show cause why he should not be reimbursed for all the costs, disbursements, and attorney fees, incurred by him in connection with the litigation involving the oil companies' attempt to remove the action to federal court.


OVERVIEW: The owner filed a petition with the trial court for a rule to show cause why a complaint he proposed to file against the oil companies should not remain within the exclusive jurisdiction of the state trial court. The complaint, not filed but attached to the owner's petition, charged the oil companies with common law conspiracy in restraint of trade. The rule was granted and proceedings stayed, but two days earlier the oil companies removed the action to federal court. At that time the oil companies filed a bond, which was conditioned that the oil companies would pay all costs and disbursements incurred by reason of the removal proceedings should it be determined that the case was improperly removed. The action was remanded to the state trial court and the owner sought costs, fees, and disbursements, based on the bond. The court quashed the appeal because the trial court's order refusing to award the claimed costs and fees was not a final and appealable order. The order did not conclusively establish the rights of the parties. The reason was that the owner never filed his complaint. Once the petition was remanded the owner sought reimbursement without ever filing his action.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the owner's appeal from the lower court, which discharged the owner's rule upon the oil companies to show cause why he should not be reimbursed for all the costs, disbursements, and attorney fees, incurred by him in connection with the litigation involving the oil companies' attempt to remove the action to federal court.

Geary v. Retirement Board of Allegheny County


No. 61, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


426 Pa. 254; 231 A.2d 743; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 570


January 9, 1967, Argued  
June 29, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 31, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, July T., 1966, No. 3530, in case of William H. Geary, Jr. v. Retirement Board of Allegheny County.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police officer appealed from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his action in mandamus seeking to compel defendant county retirement board (board) to grant his application for retirement benefits pursuant to 16 P.S. § 4710(b) (Supp. 1967).


OVERVIEW: The officer worked for the county police force for over 33 years until age 57, at which point he notified the board of his intention to retire voluntarily. The board denied his application for retirement benefits and the officer instituted a mandamus action to compel the board to grant the application. The trial court dismissed the action, holding that 16 P.S. § 4710(b) (Supp. 1967) violated due process and the prohibitions of the impairment of the obligation of contracts. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the legislation was constitutional. The court concluded that a decrease in actuarial soundness was by itself insufficient to constitute a deprivation of vested rights or an impairment of contractual rights. Further, the court found a rational basis for permitting policemen to retire five years earlier than other county employees and accordingly, denied the equal protection challenge to the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's dismissal of the police officer's action in mandamus seeking to compel the board to grant his application for retirement benefits.

 

222 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Cement Nat'l Bank v. Department of Banking


No. 74, May Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 554; 230 A.2d 209; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 713


January 13, 1967, Argued  
May 24, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused June 26, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Department of Banking, entered September 20, 1966, in case of Cement National Bank, Merchants National Bank of Allentown, and Walnutport State Bank v. Department of Banking, G. Allen Patterson, Secretary of Banking and Tri-County State Bank.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank challenged an order of appellee department of banking (department) which had granted a branch bank's application filed pursuant to 7. P.S. § 904(b)(iv) of the Pennsylvania Banking Code of 1965.


OVERVIEW: The bank claimed that the department did not have the authority to deny it the right to inspect the branch bank's application and supporting material, and that the department's refusal to hold hearings and to make findings of fact violated the bank's procedural rights, property rights, and other constitutional rights. The court disagreed with each claim and affirmed the order that approved the branch bank's application. The court held that 7 P.S. § 905(a), (c) of the Banking Code of 1965 specifically left to the department's discretion whether to hold a hearing. From those provisions, the court reasoned that the non-requirement for a hearing was based upon the legislature's recognizing the sensitive nature of the confidentiality of banking information. Further, § 905(c) also disclosed that the only requirement upon the department to make findings of fact was when it disapproved an application for a branch bank.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the department which had found there was a need for a branch bank at a certain location, and that the department's decision was not an abuse of discretion or an error of law.

 

223 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Washington Park, Inc. Appeal


Nos. 225, 235, March Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 349; 229 A.2d 1; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 687


October 5, 1966, Argued  
May 3, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Jan. T., 1966, No. 89, in re condemnation of right of way Legislative Route 62214, Section 1-A, and Legislative Route 108 in South Strabane Township.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed in appeal No. 225; appeal No. 235 quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an eminent domain proceeding, condemnee shopping center appealed from the dismissal by the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County (Pennsylvania) of the condemnee's preliminary objections to the condemnation of its land under the Eminent Domain Code of 1964, 26 P. S. § 1-101 et seq. (Supp. 1966), alleging that the taking was actually for a private rather than a public purpose. One of the condemnee's tenants also appealed.


OVERVIEW: The commonwealth had condemned part of the condemnee's land in order to provide access lanes to an interstate, which in turn required widening the road in front of the condemnee's property. The condemnee filed preliminary objections alleging that the taking was an unconstitutional exercise of the commonwealth's power of eminent domain because the taking was in fact for a private rather than a public purpose. It was undisputed that a rival shopping center would benefit from the project. The trial court concluded that the condemnee had failed to substantiate its charges with even a scintilla of evidence of fraud, corruption or misfeasance. The trial court's conclusions were approved by an en banc panel, and the court likewise affirmed. The condemnee had not met its burden to overcome the presumption that the highway department's officials had performed their duties in good faith. Further, the taking did not lose its public character merely because the rival shopping center stood to gain. The court also quashed the appeal of one of the condemnee's tenants because it was potentially entitled to file its own objections to the condemnation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing the condemnee's preliminary objections to the taking of its land for a highway construction project. The court quashed the appeal of the condemnee's tenant.

 

224 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Staino v. Cavell


No. 81, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 365; 228 A.2d 647; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 688


October 3, 1966, Argued  
April 24, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1965, No. 837, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County, July T., 1965, No. 195, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Ralph Staino, Jr. v. A. C. Cavell, Superintendent.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order denying defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus following his conviction for larceny and burglary.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial, the Commonwealth admitted evidence of defendant's silence at an interrogation hearing for the purpose of showing that defendant had tacitly admitted the truth of a statement implicating defendant in the burglary. The court noted that, at the time of the trial, the doctrine of tacit admissions was judicially accepted in the Commonwealth, and the Miranda proscriptions against the evidentiary use of such admissions did not have to be applied where the judgment against defendant had become finalized prior to the Miranda ruling. In addition, defendant was not prejudiced due to the absence of counsel when the implicating statement was presented to him, nor was he deprived of the right to confront the individual making the statement because the credibility of the individual was not an issue under the tacit admission rule. The record did not support defendant's claim that he was coerced into making a confession. The court thus concluded that defendant was not entitled to a writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order upholding the dismissal of defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

225 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Newbold v. Osser


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 478; 230 A.2d 54; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 704


Argued April 18, 1967  
April 19, 1967; May 24, 1967


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 314 and 319, Jan. T., 1967, from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1966, No. 3448, in case of Arthur E. Newbold, III, Cynthia T. Smith, Robert L. Leonard et al. v. Maurice S. Osser, Thomas P. McHenry, Louis Menna, City Commissioners, et al. Decree reversed. 479

Equity. Before GRIFFITHS, J.

Ordinance of City Council of Philadelphia apportioning City into ten councilmanic districts declared invalid, and order entered directing that all district councilmen shall be nominated and elected from the City of Philadelphia at large. Defendants appealed.



DISPOSITION: Thus we restate here our order of April 19, 1967 that the decree below is reversed and each party is to pay his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Having already reversed a decree by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which was rendered for appellee claimants against appellants, a city and its council members, the court filed its opinion. The lower court declared that an ordinance apportioning the city into districts was unconstitutional and that all council members were to be nominated and elected from the city at large pending new and valid legislation.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance was adopted pursuant to Philadelphia, Pa., Home Rule Charter § 2-102, and the realignment of the city's wards changed ward boundaries and designations and the number of wards. The city solicitor advised that redistricting by the city council was required in sufficient time to accommodate upcoming primary elections, and when the claimants submitted an alternative redistricting plan, they conceded that the ordinance, as introduced, was not objectionable as to equality of population. The bill was adopted without the proposed alterations. The court found an abuse of discretion in aborting an electoral process already in motion and found that at least one legislative attempt at revision was always allowed, even where an imminent election was to be held under an invalid plan. The court concluded that the ordinance did not deviate enough from the substantial equality of population tests in the state and federal reapportionment decisions to require inquiries into compactness, preservation of historical or physical boundaries, or gerrymandering and that the alternative plan was not significantly closer in equality of population than the redistricting ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the challenged decree.

Montella v. Camillo

Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 199; 228 A.2d 775; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 666


January 11, 1967, Argued

Rettig v. Board of County Comm'rs


No. 168, March Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 274; 228 A.2d 747; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 679


November 23, 1966, Argued  
April 18, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Dec. T., 1965, No. 4, in case of Harold Rettig, on behalf of himself and all other residents and property owners of the County of Butler similarly situated v. The Board of County Commissioners of Butler County, James A. Green, William H. McCune et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens challenged a decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County (Pennsylvania), which upheld the constitutionality of the Community College Act of 1963, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, §§ 5202-14.


OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the statute violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 20, which prohibited the legislature from delegating any power to levy taxes or perform any municipal function. The court found that when first enacted, the Community College Act provided that a school district could sponsor such school and enter into contracts that would be binding upon the local sponsor. This had previously been found to be unconstitutional because it gave a board of trustees unlimited power to incur debts. The legislature then amended the statute by § 2 of the amending Act of October 26, 1965, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 5203(b). The court held that the legislature had the power to provide for a system of community colleges; only its delegation of authority in one aspect of so doing was at issue. The 1965 amendment was curative legislation designed to erase a supposed defect in the Act. The legislature was competent to enact such curative legislation. Under the amended statute, all fiscal authority was vested in the local sponsor; administration of the operations of the community college was vested in the board of trustees, which posed no constitutional issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree.

 

228 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Mount v. Rundle


No. 367, January Term, 1965


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 312; 228 A.2d 640; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 684


November 18, 1966, Argued  
April 18, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas No. 2 of Philadelphia County, June T., 1965, No. 1012, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. George A. Mount v. Alfred T. Rundle, Superintendent.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. *



* Reporter's Note: On October 9, 1967, the Supreme Court of the United States denied petition for a writ of certiorari.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted of murder and sentenced to death, sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas No. 2 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his petition for a writ of habeas corpus, in which defendant contended that his sentence was illegal and violative of his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Following defendant's arrest, he admitted stabbing the victim. Defendant made a second statement after a preliminary hearing. The court disagreed with defendant's argument that the second statement, obtained when he was without counsel and had not been warned of his right to remain silent, should not have been admitted into evidence. The statement was made under pre-Miranda and pre-Escobedo law, and the statement was not rendered inadmissible where defendant was not shown to be prejudiced by such admission. A waiver of defendant's right to counsel had occurred because he had not requested counsel. Defendant was not denied his constitutional rights by the admission of his statement made after his preliminary hearing and prior to his representation by counsel. A preliminary hearing was not deemed to be a critical stage of the criminal proceeding. Moreover, defendant had failed to object at trial to the admission of the second statement. Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, § 784 was not violated by the failure to appoint counsel until after his indictment. Even without the second statement, however, the evidence was sufficient to justify the verdict and death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing defendant's habeas corpus petition.

Exton Quarries, Inc. v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


No. 156, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


425 Pa. 43; 228 A.2d 169; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 644


May 3, 1966, Argued  
March 14, 1967, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused April 15, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Nov. T., 1964, No. 140, in case of Exton Quarries, Incorporated v. Zoning Board of Adjustment of West Whiteland Township.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which reversed a decision of the zoning board and held that a zoning ordinance of the township that restricted quarrying within its borders was invalid as an exercise of the power granted to second class townships and unconstitutional as an exercise of police powers.


OVERVIEW: Appellee quarry, located within the township, was constructed, but not yet operational, prior to the enactment of a zoning ordinance of the township that completed banned quarrying within its borders. Pursuant to the ordinance, the township denied the quarry the right to operate its business. On appeal of the lower court's determination that the ordinance was in derogation of the Enabling Act, 53 P.S. §§ 67001-67003, as well as unconstitutional, the court ruled that the Enabling Act, which granted broad and flexible powers, did authorize township-wide bans on selected uses of land, but that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court held that a zoning ordinance which totally excludes a particular business from an entire municipality must bear a more substantial relationship to the public health, safety, morals, and general welfare than an ordinance which merely confines that business to a certain area in the municipality. The court then found that the townships objections to the quarry, such as noise, vibrations, aesthetics, were not sufficiently related to the protection of public health and safety as to sustain the constitutionality of the restriction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the lower court and found that the zoning ordinance unconstitutionally restricted the use of private property.

Borg-Warner Corp. v. Board of Finance & Revenue


Nos. 33, 34, May Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


424 Pa. 343; 227 A.2d 153; 1967 Pa. LEXIS 783


May 24, 1966, Argued  
March 14, 1967, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from orders of Board of Finance and Revenue, Docket No. M-17,740, in case of Borg-Warner Corporation v. Board of Finance and Revenue; and Docket No. M-17,808, in case of Textron, Inc. v. Same.



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Department of Revenue assessed upon appellant taxpayers additional realty transfer taxes. The taxpayers filed petitions for review with appellee Board of Finance and Revenue (board). The board denied the petitions. The parties settled the dispute. The taxpayers then filed a petition with the board for a refund. The board denied the petition. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the parties settled their dispute and after they filed their second petitions for a refund, a common pleas court issued a ruling that under the Realty Transfer Tax Act, 1951 Pa. Laws 1742, the commonwealth did not have authority to impose a transfer tax after a certain date (the very same dispute as was between the parties). Section 503 of the Fiscal Code, 72 P.S. § 503, gave the board jurisdiction to hear a petition for refund originally paid under an act or interpretation of an act later held unconstitutional. The court held that once an agreement or stipulation was entered into, a later decision more favorable to one side than the other did not allow the side so favored to withdraw from the prior agreement or stipulation and relitigate a settled issue. The taxpayers had not paid the taxes under the circumstances cited in the Fiscal Code. The taxes were paid as the result of a settlement. The stipulation stated that the taxpayers abandoned their contentions. Thus the taxpayers were bound by the stipulation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the board which assessment upon the taxpayers additional realty transfer taxes.

Act No. 91 aimed to place nonpublic schools, in the matter of student transportation, in the same classification as public schools, so long as the nonpublic schools observed the public school curricula. The Federal or State constitution, the laws of the Commonwealth, or in fundamental justice to prohibit this objective.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the associated individuals' complaints.

West Mead v. Terregino


No. 13, March Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


423 Pa. 173; 223 A.2d 745; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 448


September 29, 1966, Argued  
November 15, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Sept. T., 1964, No. 140, in case of Township of West Mead v. Anthony Terregino.



DISPOSITION: Appeal quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Township of West Mead sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County (Pennsylvania), which found appellee business owner not guilty of violating an ordinance passed pursuant to the Pennsylvania Act of March 11, 1959, P.L. 13, Penn Stat. Ann. tit. 52, § 6758. A justice of the peace had found the owner guilty of violating the ordinance, but on appeal and after a hearing, the lower court found him not guilty.


OVERVIEW: The Township had charged that the owner had operated his junk business without obtaining a license and without proper compliance with the ordinance's requirements. The lower court found the owner not guilty, concluding that the requirements of the ordinance were unconstitutional and that the Township's insistence on the owner's erecting a fence around his premises was unjustified. On further review, the court found that the Township had failed to comply with Pa. R. Civ. P. 68 =, which provided that where the subject matter of a suit did not fall within the statutory jurisdiction of a lower court, where a statute expressly provided that there would be no appeal from an order of a lower court or provided that an order would be final or conclusive or not subject to review, or where the relevant statute was silent on the question of appellate review, an appeal to the court in the nature of a certiorari from a judgment order or decree would lie only if specially allowed by the court. The court held that Rule 68 = set forth the requirements of a petition filed by a party seeking an appeal to the court, and that no such petition was filed by the Township. The court quashed the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal filed by the Township.

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: The school district sought to tax the owners under the Act of 1863. The court held that the district had not complied with § 657 of Pa. Public School Code of 1949, Pa. Stat. Commonwealth v. Murray


No. 225, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


423 Pa. 37; 223 A.2d 102; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 436


April 22, 1966, Argued  
October 6, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1965, No. 394, affirming judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County, June T., 1963, No. 1712, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. John Murray.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Superior Court is reversed, and judgment of sentence of court of quarter sessions is reversed, and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court of quarter sessions entered a judgment of conviction against defendant for offering to bribe and bribing a corporate employee. The order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed the judgment of conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contacted an employee of his former employer and asked him to obtain certain prints of a perforating machine. The employee informed his supervisors, who hired private detectives to entrap defendant. A private detective assigned to the case placed a wire tap on the telephone switchboard at the detective agency. The detective also listened on an extension phone to the employee's conversation with defendant. Defendant agreed to come to the employee's home to pick up the prints and to pay the employee for obtaining them. Defendant picked up the prints and was arrested. On appeal, defendant argued that the interception of his telephone conversation without his permission was illegal. The court found that the Act of July 16, 1957, 15 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2443, required the consent of both parties to the communication. While the employee consented, defendant did not. The commonwealth asserted that it was not illegal to listen in on a telephone extension without consent. The court disagreed because a wiretap and a telephone extension both resulted in eavesdropping, which was an unconstitutional invasion of privacy.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction against defendant was reversed and the case was remanded to the court of quarter sessions for a new trial.

 

235 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Valley Forge Golf Club v. Upper Merion Township


No. 26, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. 227; 221 A.2d 292; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 552


May 24, 1966, Argued  
June 27, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 66-1325, in case of Valley Forge Golf Club v. Upper Merion Township.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff club challenged a decree from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed a petition seeking a preliminary injunction against defendant township to restrain condemning the club's real estate. The township filed preliminary objections challenging equity jurisdiction pursuant to the Eminent Domain Code, Act of June 22, 1964, P. L. 84, § 101 et seq., Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 26, § 1-101 et seq. (Supp. 1965).


OVERVIEW: The club contended that equity had jurisdiction to enjoin the planned condemnation of its real estate because the remedy afforded by the Eminent Domain Code was inadequate under the circumstances. Allegedly when the club lost possession it would be put out of business because its business could not be relocated. The court disagreed, stating that the legislature did not contemplate a resort to equity courts by either condemnor or condemnee. The court pointed out that in the instant matter, no declaration of taking had been filed when the club began its suit in equity, although several weeks later the township's board of supervisors enacted an ordinance authorizing the filing of such a declaration. The court concluded that the Eminent Domain Code established procedures which protected and safeguarded the rights of the condemnee to the same extent as would a proceeding in equity. The court held that because the legislature provided exclusive proceedings under the Code in eminent domain cases, no remedy in equity was available to an injured property owner such as the club.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decree dismissing a petition for a preliminary injunction against the township. The court assigned costs to the club.

 

236 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Saddler v. Maroney


No. 39, May Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. 13; 220 A.2d 846; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 517


May 23, 1966, Submitted  
June 24, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, June T., 1965, No. 1740, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Andrew Lee Saddler v. James F. Maroney, Superintendent.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The inmate, while represented by privately obtained counsel, pled guilty to and was convicted of murder in the first degree and was sentenced to life in prison. He did not appeal the conviction or file any post-conviction motions. Years later, the inmate filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, which the trial court denied. Upon review the court affirmed the trial court's order. The court held that because the inmate failed, at the trial level, to attack the voluntariness of his confession, he could not attack it years later in connection with an habeas corpus petition. The court found that because the inmate signed a guilty plea that informed him that he was waiving his right to a jury trial, his contention that he was not informed of his right to a jury trial was without merit. The court determined that there was no evidence that the inmate's counsel failed to adequately inform him that he had the right to appeal his conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

 

237 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Ametrane


No. 74, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. 83; 221 A.2d 296; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 528


April 21, 1966, Argued  
June 24, 1966, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 25, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1964, Nos. 582 and 583, affirming judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Delaware County, Dec. T., 1963, Nos. 110 and 111, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Joseph Ametrane.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a lower court's conviction and sentence against him for setting up a gambling establishment and aiding and assisting others to gamble and bookmaking. Defendant argued that certain evidence should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: Police officers searched defendant's home with two search warrants and one arrest warrant. The police seized various evidence, which was used to convict defendant of setting up a gambling establishment and aiding and assisting others to gamble and bookmaking. The superior court affirmed the conviction and sentence. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence should have been suppressed because the police officers failed to announce their identity and purpose prior to entry of his house. The court disagreed and affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence. The court held that defendant's Fourth Amendment rights were not violated. The court noted that defendant saw the officers approach his door, defendant initially did not respond to the officers' knock, and when the officers indicated that they would use a crowbar, defendant opened the door. The court held that under these circumstances, it was obvious that defendant knew the officers' identity and their purpose without a formal announcement. The court concluded that the warrants were sufficient and that the search was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order.

Price v. Philadelphia Parking Authority


No. 252, January Term, 1965


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. 317; 221 A.2d 138; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 568


June 29, 1965, Argued  
June 24, 1966, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued April 19, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas No. 1 of Philadelphia County, March T., 1964, No. 5376, in case of B. Price and Dr. Barook Masuda v. The Philadelphia Parking Authority.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the decree of the Court of Common Pleas, No. 1 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied their action to enjoin appellee public corporation from proceeding under agreements to build parking projects.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged the legality of appellee's two projects alleging that appellee, by employing negotiated agreements rather than competitive bidding, had exceeded its statutory authority. The trial court granted appellee's motion to dismiss with respect to one project on the ground that appellants lacked standing to challenge the transaction and found that appellee had proceeded correctly with respect to the other project. On appeal, the court found that appellants, as representatives of the taxpayers of the City of Philadelphia, possessed the requisite pecuniary interest in the projects to challenge the transaction. The court found that appellee was not free to dispense with competitive bidding in the leasing of air rights and held that the agreement entered into by private negotiation rather than by competitive bidding was unauthorized and void. The court reversed the decree finding that the trial court erred in denying appellants injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree finding that appellants had standing to challenge the agreements of appellee public corporation and that a public corporation was required to proceed by competitive bidding.

 

239 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Webb v. Zern


No. 297, January Term, 1965


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. 424; 220 A.2d 853; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 572


November 12, 1965, Argued  
June 24, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Dec. T., 1964, No. 28, in case of Nelson R. Webb v. John A. Zern, D. G. Yuengling & Son, Inc. and The Benson Manufacturing Co., Inc.



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff litigant was severely injured when a keg of beer, which his father had purchased and his brother had tapped, exploded. The litigant brought an action for damages against defendants, the beer distributor, the brewer who filled the keg, and the manufacturer of the keg. The Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) sustained defendants' demurrers and dismissed the complaint. The litigant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The litigant relied on a theory of exclusive control, averring in his complaint that he had no knowledge of the cause of the explosion or of which party defendant was responsible for the explosion. The trial court held that the doctrine was inapplicable because the litigant had failed to join his father and brother as defendants. The court said, however, that it did not need to determine whether or not the trial court erred with regard to the law of exclusive control because the court adopted a new basis for liability in Pennsylvania, which was strict liability in tort for defective products. The court held that one who sold any product in a defective condition unreasonably dangerous to the user or consumer or to his property was subject to liability for physical harm thereby caused to the ultimate user or consumer, or to his property, if (a) the seller was engaged in the business of selling such a product, and (b) the product was expected to and did reach the user or consumer without substantial change in the condition in which it was sold. The court stated that the rule applied even where the seller had exercised all possible care in the preparation and sale of his product.


OUTCOME: The appellate court vacated the order that dismissed the litigant's complaint, permitting the litigant to amend his complaint to explicitly state a cause of action in trespass for defective products liability.

Bell Tel. Co. v. Philadelphia


No. 326, January Term, 1965


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


421 Pa. 14; 218 A.2d 727; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 605


January 5, 1966, Argued  
March 22, 1966, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused May 4, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas No. 2 of Philadelphia County, March T., 1953, No. 3922, in case of The Bell Telephone Company of Pennsylvania v. City of Philadelphia, George S. Forde, Revenue Commissioner, and Walter Pytko, Commissioner of Licenses and Inspections, of the City of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant telephone company sought review of a decree entered by the Court of Common Pleas No. 2 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which held that its original decree, which enjoined appellee City of Philadelphia from enforcing a tax known as the Mercantile License Tax as to the telephone company, was in error and which modified the decree as to restrain the enforcement only for certain years preceding and including 1953.


OVERVIEW: Under the Sterling Act the City adopted an ordinance imposing the Mercantile License Tax upon persons engaged in certain business activities. The telephone company and others successfully sought a decree in equity prohibiting as to them imposition of the Tax. The decree perpetually enjoined the City from enforcing or attempting to enforce the Tax against the company. The City later assessed the Tax against certain of the telephone company's incidental receipts. When it was held that the City's assessment violated the decree, the City petitioned for a modification of the injunction. The lower court granted the petition and held that the decree, which enjoined collection of the Tax perpetually, was in error. The lower court also held that the question as to whether the company's incidental receipts were subject to the Tax was a proper one for consideration by the Philadelphia Tax Review Board. The court found that an erroneous interpretation of a taxing statute did not result in the statute not being enforced again against the decree winner. The court also rejected the company's objections to equity's referral of the matter to the Tax Review Board. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decree.

County of Chester v. Philadelphia Electric Co.


No. 42, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


420 Pa. 422; 218 A.2d 331; 1966 Pa. LEXIS 779


January 7, 1966, Argued  
March 22, 1966, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 1716 of 1964, in equity, in case of The County of Chester v. The Philadelphia Electric Company and Henkels & McCoy, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) which awarded judgment to appellee electric company by declaring an ordinance it had enacted unconstitutional. The court found that the disputed ordinance was unconstitutional under the Public Utility Law, Act of May 28, 1937, 1937 P. L. 1053, § 1 et seq., as amended ( Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 66, § 1101 et seq.)


OVERVIEW: The county enacted an ordinance which prohibited persons or corporations from constructing pipelines without submitting to it plans related to the routes and use of such lines. The electric company refused to be bound by the provisions of that ordinance and began construction of a pipeline project. By response the county instituted an action in equity to enjoin the company from pursuing that construction job. The county's ordinance was declared unconstitutional by the trial court and the court affirmed its decision. Counties were subdivisions of the State and enjoyed no sovereign power to legislate on statewide matters. Counties could not legislate independently of a Commonwealth's declared policy. Under the Public Utility Law, Act of May 28, 1937, 1937 P. L. 1053, § 1 et seq., as amended ( Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 66, § 1101 et seq.) the Public Utility Commission had regulatory jurisdiction over companies. Courts would not originally adjudicate matters within the Commission's jurisdiction, including matters related to the service of power. If the county objected to the company's actions its remedy was to ask the Commission for appropriate regulatory relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision which found that the county had acted improperly.
